        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer565641813">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16202413872437172037">This novel takes place in 1964 during the Civil Rights Act in Southern Carolina.<br />Lily Owen is a fourteen year old poor Southern white girl who lives on an isolated peach farm with her abusive father T. Ray and Rosaleen the big hearted black housekeeper. Lily's mother died tragically when she was four years old. Lily has grown up believing that she was the one who accidentally killed her mother with a shotgun. Lily spends most her days working hard on the farm with no appreciation or love from her</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16202413872437172037">This novel takes place in 1964 during the Civil Rights Act in Southern Carolina.<br />Lily Owen is a fourteen year old poor Southern white girl who lives on an isolated peach farm with her abusive father T. Ray and Rosaleen the big hearted black housekeeper. Lily's mother died tragically when she was four years old. Lily has grown up believing that she was the one who accidentally killed her mother with a shotgun. Lily spends most her days working hard on the farm with no appreciation or love from her father. Lily and Rosaleen get along quite well together and it's just as well seeing as Lily has no one else in her life that she she can share her feelings or thoughts with.<br /><br />One afternoon Rosaleen goes into town to register herself to vote, but ends up getting into a fight with three male racists and she ends up being beaten and sent to jail. Rosaleen is Lily's only one true friend and knows she must do something so she ends up getting Rosaleen out of jail and together they decide to runaway.<br />They end up living with Boatwright sisters May, June and August who run and maintain a bee keeping business. Whilst living with the Boatwright sisters Lily is shown and taught by August on how the business is run and just what goes into making honey and bees wax products.<br />In the months that follow we see how Lily and Rosaleen start to experience what it's like to live in a family of love and friendship.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book and I really liked the little facts about bees at the start of each chapter. This was a moving story of friendship, racism and forgiveness and one in which I'm sure many people will enjoy reading.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16202413872437172037">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_17144154">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17144154" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/644585-morgan" class="left imgcol" title="Morgan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1307978650p2/644585.jpg" alt="Morgan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/17144154?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 15, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/644585-morgan" class="user" title="Morgan">Morgan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Southern women who haven't read enough Southern women books</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17144154">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3655046955856221341">Being from the South, I am quite familiar with the whole "Southern girl coming of age" genre. I have read many of these books, and the best are framed with nuanced, real characters that raise the book from cliche (Bastard Out of Carolina is a good example of this). This was not one of those books.<br /><br />I am not totally blasting this book--much of it was written well, and parts were almost enjoyable. Lily was (most of the time) a pretty likable character, and I actually cared about her during the book.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3655046955856221341">Being from the South, I am quite familiar with the whole "Southern girl coming of age" genre. I have read many of these books, and the best are framed with nuanced, real characters that raise the book from cliche (Bastard Out of Carolina is a good example of this). This was not one of those books.<br /><br />I am not totally blasting this book--much of it was written well, and parts were almost enjoyable. Lily was (most of the time) a pretty likable character, and I actually cared about her during the book. I also liked Rosaleen's character, although I thought she was a bit oversimplified, and I really wanted to hear more about that husband of hers...<br /><br />But this book was just really cliche. Despite the good writing in sections, the characters were unable to overcome the very simplistic treatment they were given. August, for example, was a character who could have been strong, yet ended up feeling very one-dimensional. She was never shown to have any real faults--was she even human? Even Lily suffered from a lack of little human touches--we know she had a cuticle problem (like most girls in Southern coming of age stories for some reason), but nothing really drew her as a complicated person except for her relationship with her mother and father.<br /><br />And one thing just irked me the whole book. Why did T Ray have her kneel on grits? Yeah, it would probably be painful, but how many bags of grits was this guy wasting by doing this? Maybe I'm not an expert on middle 20th century Southern farmers, but I imagine he would be a bit more economical than this.<br /><br />Overall, I barely got through this one, and I complained about it the whole time.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3655046955856221341">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14012448193747805428">There are books you read along the way that take your breath away. This was definetly one of them for me. I truly connected with the main character Lily toward the end of the book. On page 278 was when my breath was taken, when tears sprang from my eyes, and I thought this could be me talking at 14 years old. It read .."I wished she'd been smart enough, or loving enough, to realize everybody has burdens that crush them, only they don't give up thier children. In a weird way I must have loved my</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14012448193747805428">There are books you read along the way that take your breath away. This was definetly one of them for me. I truly connected with the main character Lily toward the end of the book. On page 278 was when my breath was taken, when tears sprang from my eyes, and I thought this could be me talking at 14 years old. It read .."I wished she'd been smart enough, or loving enough, to realize everybody has burdens that crush them, only they don't give up thier children. In a weird way I must have loved my little collection of hurts and wounds. They provided me some real nice sympathy, with the feeling I was exceptional. I was the girl abandoned by her mother." To say more about this would only create more tears so I will move on. I will say I love a great line in a book. The ones I could read again and again and often times write down simply because it touches me, makes me think, makes me cry, or makes me laugh. There were many in this book but the one I will end with is a quote from August, "People can start out one way, and by the time life gets through with them they end up completely different." I continue to be molded by lifes circumstances (and the books I read)! Thanks Jen for recommending such a great read!</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_6715745">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6715745" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/413529-hannah" class="left imgcol" title="Hannah"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Hannah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6715745?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 26, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/413529-hannah" class="user" title="Hannah">Hannah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6715745">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1895200088645805590">Reading the Secret Life of Bees is like meeting the best friend of your childhood within the confines of 302 pages of prose. Lily Owens is a 14-year-old girl who only wants a place to belong after running away from her miserable father with her black nursemaid Rosaleen. With nowhere else to go, Lily and Rosaleen are taken in by three eccentric sisters: black beekeepers who hold the clues to Lily’s past, and quite possibly her future. Expertly set in 1964 in the heart of the American South, Lily</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1895200088645805590">Reading the Secret Life of Bees is like meeting the best friend of your childhood within the confines of 302 pages of prose. Lily Owens is a 14-year-old girl who only wants a place to belong after running away from her miserable father with her black nursemaid Rosaleen. With nowhere else to go, Lily and Rosaleen are taken in by three eccentric sisters: black beekeepers who hold the clues to Lily’s past, and quite possibly her future. Expertly set in 1964 in the heart of the American South, Lily witnesses the everyday atrocities of a society in which color matters and fairness doesn’t. <br /><br />Lily’s narrative voice is so strong in this piece that we are taken through her world as if it were our own. Lily desperately wants to be understood and through her moving narration we live her life and understand. We wince as her father makes her kneel on dry grits. We hold our breaths as she breaks Rosaleen out of jail for standing up to a racist policeman. We feel her longing as she searches for clues to her mother’s past. We are ashamed as she discovers the prejudices she harbors against her new friends. We wonder as bees keep popping up in her life and become intertwined with her quest to find a home. We smile as she learns to find happiness. The exceptional setting and sensory cues place you in her world, and Lily’s voice places you in her life. True and insightful, conflicted and strong, she is certainly one of the best narrators I have ever had the pleasure of knowing.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1895200088645805590">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a href="/book/show/6288887-la-vita-segreta-delle-api" title="Brossura 9788804569008" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer170233947">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11357143396893754947">Immagina che tu ed io, tu che leggi, e io che scrivo il commento, siamo amici migliori da un triliardo di anni, di quelli che chiamano ‘zio’ e ‘zia’ i rispettivi genitori, che sanno che metti a lavare le cose che non vuoi ripiegare e riporre che nei cassetti, che io ti abbia visto sbavare/morire d’amore/penare per un amore non corrisposto, oppure del tutto immaginario, che ti abbia accompagnato al funerale di un nonno piangendo lacrime amare oppure di un trisavolo antiquato con tanto di risate d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11357143396893754947">Immagina che tu ed io, tu che leggi, e io che scrivo il commento, siamo amici migliori da un triliardo di anni, di quelli che chiamano ‘zio’ e ‘zia’ i rispettivi genitori, che sanno che metti a lavare le cose che non vuoi ripiegare e riporre che nei cassetti, che io ti abbia visto sbavare/morire d’amore/penare per un amore non corrisposto, oppure del tutto immaginario, che ti abbia accompagnato al funerale di un nonno piangendo lacrime amare oppure di un trisavolo antiquato con tanto di risate di circostanza, insomma, che ci sia stata per te dalla notte dei tempi e t’abbia curato le ferite che quando sono tue sono un po’ anche le mie.<br /><br />Adesso ti vedo che giungi ad un punto cruciale della tua esistenza, ti vedo aggirarti con un punto di domanda sulla faccia o la lacrima persistente dell’ultimo pianto che stenta ad andare via, ti sto a sentire mentre parli, ma forse in fondo anche a me stavolta mancano i mezzi per aiutarti davvero perché purtroppo non s’è mai saputo di una persona che entrasse dentro un’altra e andasse a tirar via i cocci dell’ennesimo danno che la vita ha rotto dentro di te. Però un modo per rompere le barriere di sofferenza lo trovo, almeno per alleviarle, si lo trovo, e questo modo si chiama ‘La vita segreta delle api’, un libro. Sei scettico, amico mio, riguardo la lettura e la sua funzione salvifica, e probabilmente chiusa pagina 291 e dato un veloce sguardo ai riferimenti bibliografici starai male come prima, se non peggio perché non c’hai manco un’amica decente che ti sappia aiutare. Ma probabilmente non andrà così.<br /><br />Con me non è andata così. Quando hai 20 anni e letteralmente non sai che pesci pigliare perché un voto di un esame è troppo basso, il fidanzato ha troppe ore di facoltà, senti la nostalgia di casa, ma in fondo non ci torneresti mai perché raggiunta l’indipendenza non torneresti mai più indietro, ti senti niente più e niente meno che un cappotto vuoto. Solo che tu nemmeno hai la gruccia che ti tieni in piedi dentro all’armadio. Allora mi son presa un libro, io lettrice, che i libri mi fanno da mamma, da amica, da compagna di risate e tutto quanto.<br /><br />Ma non ho preso un libro a caso, ne ho preso uno dal quale mi aspettavo ‘Una storia commovente ed umana che mi faccia provare quanto dolce può essere la vita’. Proprio così, sulla mia bizzarra scheda libro ho inserito una voce<br /><br />ASPETTATIVE: ’Una storia commovente ed umana che mi faccia provare quanto dolce può essere la vita’<br /><br />E le aspettative sono state ripagate, e superate da una storia che ti cola nella gola come il miele che ti cura il male facendoti sentire un po’ rinvigorito, cullato e coccolato dalla dolcezza di questo prodotto che producono le api nel loro alveare. La vita in fondo è un’ape, no? A volte ci punge, a volte ci delizia col miele. Ma la cosa più importante, la lezione più bella ricevuta, è stata quella , sì, di lasciarsi cullare e coccolare, ma anche di combattere, di sentirsi sempre pronti al rilancio, alla rivincita, al momento del ‘Adesso chiedo alla vita ciò che mi merito’. Potreste essere Lily, una bianca in mezzo ai negri che va in cerca delle orme di una madre morta, troppo viva nel ricordo, e che prega la Madonna Nera, che le chiede di lanciarle un’ancora di salvataggio; potreste essere Rosaleen, che dopo il 1964 e l’Atto sui Diritti Civili va orgogliosa a ritirare la sua tessera elettorale anche se dopo essere stata picchiata; potreste essere Zach, ragazzino col sogno di divenire avvocato, che va al liceo dei bianchi e se ne infischia delle palline di carta.<br /><br />Potreste anche essere August, e piegarvi al mistero della vita per dedicarvi all’umanità, alle vostre sorelle, alle vostre amiche care. Oppure potreste essere May, la dolce gemella a metà che un giorno ha smesso di sopportare il peso del mondo.<br /><br />Chiunque decidiate di essere, dopo aver letto questa storia, tuttavia avrete voglia di guardare il sole nel cielo, pensare che splende per voi e che finalmente è giunta l’ora di rinascere.<br /><br />Dunque, amico mio inventato sul momento, o amica vera che passi per ‘Un buon libro, un ottimo amico’ a vedere che cosa fa Luana, se ha scritto qualcosa di interessante, se stai male, per qualsiasi cosa, se ti senti uno sconfitto, chiedi a Sue Monk Kidd di darti in prestito i suoi personaggi. Infilati nella Carolina del Sud, ammira i segreti delle api, impara la forza dell’amore materno, fonte del mondo, ricevi silenzioso la lezione sul colore della pelle, che è una lezione di intelligenza e di sapienza del persistere del principio che siamo tutti uguali, che bianchi, o neri, o svolazzanti come api non abbiamo che l’amore a farci da guida.<br /><br />Adesso posso anche finire di emozionarmi, e iniziare a sperare di averti convinto che talvolta un libro e delle api in un alveare possono restituire un sorriso e il caldo abbraccio dell’affetto.<br /><br />Post Scriptum: Esiste del libro una meravigliosa trasposizione cinematografica omonima che vi consiglio di guardare.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=yifIR42rpNc" target="_blank">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=yifIR4...</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11357143396893754947">...more</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4693" class="user" title="???">???</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4693?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21380826">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7988264172594502198">Thanks a lot to Sue Monk Kido: “Words are the most beautiful things existed in the world, but they die as fast as they were born, unless you convert them to act”! This is so simple but a fact, as beautiful and strong as life.<br />The Secret life of Bees is a large combination of beautiful moments, scenes, words, principles and characters, such as May, the one sister who “has no walls around her heart”, and the description of “wailing wall” where one deal with her / his own suffering. I love what Augu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7988264172594502198">Thanks a lot to Sue Monk Kido: “Words are the most beautiful things existed in the world, but they die as fast as they were born, unless you convert them to act”! This is so simple but a fact, as beautiful and strong as life.<br />The Secret life of Bees is a large combination of beautiful moments, scenes, words, principles and characters, such as May, the one sister who “has no walls around her heart”, and the description of “wailing wall” where one deal with her / his own suffering. I love what August would call “Bee yard etiquettes; “Don’t be afraid as no life – loving bee wants to sting you. Still, don’t be idiot, wear long sleeves and long pants … if you feel angry, whistle. Anger agitates, while whistling melts a bee’s temper. Act you know what you’re doing, even if you don’t”. yeah! absolutment! And above all, send the bees love. Every little thing wants to be loved”! that’s above the 10 commands! and beyond every other etiquettes!<br />I would jump up and down of joy, when August said: “Stories have to be told or they die, and where they die, we can’t remember who we are, or why we’re here”. This is simply a verse, and I consider the whole book as a long beautiful poem, narrated by a lady.<br />I love the idea that May, July and August (the three sisters) are free of any restriction during their own month; May in May, June in June and August in August, I’ll try to remember August, as this is my month, too! Am absolutely agree with August saying “Everything needs a God who looks like them” that’s why that black people like black Madonna”! What wonderful stories August is telling about her mother, specially that where her mother tease Mary Madonna saying; “you know what? You should have had a girl instead”! <br />I lived with the three sisters in the bee-house / pink house while reading the novel. And I should confess that I fell in love with August, such a remarkable lady, living Madonna: “Every person on the face of the earth makes mistakes, Lily. Every last one. We’re all so human, … there is nothing perfect, there is life”!<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9499184548655329856">I listened to this book while jogging. It's a wonderful story to listen to while out on the trails. The story slowly unfolds and the underlying fears are touching and you want to wrap Lily up in your arms and keep her safe. <br />Lily's voice is wonderful. Her views are funny, realistic and hopeful. Never does she lose hope and she always tries so hard to find the good. <br />In the end, this book is about being protected, loving &amp; being loved and forgiveness. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_18222669">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18222669" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/419071-mayla" class="left imgcol" title="Mayla"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1284872170p2/419071.jpg" alt="Mayla" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18222669?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/419071-mayla" class="user" title="Mayla">Mayla</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18222669">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10607721970088509187"><u>The Secret Life Of Bees</u><br />Sue Monk Kidd<br /><br />Penguin Group, 2003, $14.00, 336 pgs.<br />ISBN 9-78-014200-1<br /><br /> “Most people don’t have any idea about all the complicated life going on inside a hive. Bees have a secret life we don’t know anything about” (Kidd 148). The Secret Life Of Bees allows us to discover more about creatures we think less of. Bees – what do they do? Disgusting and small, how complicated can things be for them? All they do is make honey and buzz, being sticky and annoying. That was what I thou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10607721970088509187"><u>The Secret Life Of Bees</u><br />Sue Monk Kidd<br /><br />Penguin Group, 2003, $14.00, 336 pgs.<br />ISBN 9-78-014200-1<br /><br /> “Most people don’t have any idea about all the complicated life going on inside a hive. Bees have a secret life we don’t know anything about” (Kidd 148). The Secret Life Of Bees allows us to discover more about creatures we think less of. Bees – what do they do? Disgusting and small, how complicated can things be for them? All they do is make honey and buzz, being sticky and annoying. That was what I thought, but there is more to this secret life of bees inside their hive.<br />The author, Sue Monk Kidd has a very unique realistic story. What’s even more special and different about her book, are her themes – how she uses bees to compare it with Lily’s (our protagonist of the story) situations. It’s not hard to run away from home knowing your father will make you kneel on grits every chance he gets. That’s what our 14-year old teenage, Lily is thinking – She is never going back. She decides to run away resentfully and unforgiving (her father). With her loyal companion who has taken care of her all her life, together, they determinedly travel to their pinpoint. The one place that holds the secrets of Lily’s past – the pink house of the three sisters (August, June, and May).<br />When I first picked up this book, I felt that it was foolish; a book about bees? When I first started reading it, it was just about an innocent girl who just loved the buzzing sound of bees. Even though, the author started the story out with our boring dozy protagonist, she doesn’t wait for long to jump into the story. Meaningful and unique, this is by far one of the best books I’ve read in a very long time.<br /><br />- Mayla Wang 903<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10607721970088509187">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/21992464-helen-the-bookowl" class="left imgcol" title="helen the bookowl"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451068274p2/21992464.jpg" alt="helen the bookowl" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1328804035?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/21992464-helen-the-bookowl" class="user" title="helen the bookowl">helen the bookowl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1328804035">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10769224507478315976">This was a funny and sweet story mixed with the serious topic of the Civil Rights Agreement of the 1960s in USA. The protagonist, Lily, is such a lovable character because she's a child and perceives the world as the 14-year-old she is, but at the same time she goes through so many brutal things that your heart bleeds for her. Her father, T-Ray,is obviously a complete jerk and I had no problems feeling absolutely no sympathy for him. His name made me think of T Rex - the dinosaur. <br />While I did lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10769224507478315976">This was a funny and sweet story mixed with the serious topic of the Civil Rights Agreement of the 1960s in USA. The protagonist, Lily, is such a lovable character because she's a child and perceives the world as the 14-year-old she is, but at the same time she goes through so many brutal things that your heart bleeds for her. Her father, T-Ray,is obviously a complete jerk and I had no problems feeling absolutely no sympathy for him. His name made me think of T Rex - the dinosaur. <br />While I did love this endearing story which starts out light and becomes gradually more heavy, I also felt like some of the things happening were unrealistic and too silly. I liked that this story was funny, but I didn't really feel like it fit the overall mood and theme of the book. I'm sure Sue Monk Kidd was trying to write about a serious topic but with a funny take, but I think I personally would have appreciated the book more if it had sustained the serious tone and mood throughout. <br />I read this book in a day because it was so easily read. It's perfect for the summer because it takes place in the blazing heat of the southern state of Carolina, but also because it is humorous and fluffy at times. I really liked this book because it put a smile to my face while also breaking my heart a little bit, and I definitely recommend it. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10769224507478315976">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {

      return

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }

      }

Worse, the Sweet Stuff Bakery may be closing, and a suspicious real estate agent is trying to turn the beloved house on the hill into a spa. Can change be coming to Mitford? The buzz on Main Street says yes. Change is certainly coming to the tenderest regions of several townspeople\'s lives. A woman struggles every day to stay on course after years of hard living. A man tries to forgive himself for a tragic mistake.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4711.Sue_Monk_Kidd">About Sue Monk Kidd</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/4711.Sue_Monk_Kidd"><img alt="4711" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1387316516p2/4711.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer7711122023049761996"><br /><strong>SUE MONK KIDD</strong> was raised in the small town of Sylvester, Georgia. She graduated from Texas Christian University in 1970 and later took creative writing courses at Emory University and Anderson College, as well as studying at Sewanee, Bread Loaf, and other writers’ conferences. In her forties, Kidd turned her attention to writing fiction, winning the South Carolina Fellowship in Literature and the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7711122023049761996"><br /><strong>SUE MONK KIDD</strong> was raised in the small town of Sylvester, Georgia. She graduated from Texas Christian University in 1970 and later took creative writing courses at Emory University and Anderson College, as well as studying at Sewanee, Bread Loaf, and other writers’ conferences. In her forties, Kidd turned her attention to writing fiction, winning the South Carolina Fellowship in Literature and the 1996 Poets &amp; Writers Exchange Program in Fiction.<br /><br />When her first novel, <em>The Secret Life of Bees</em>, was published by Viking in 2002, it became a genuine literary phenomenon, spending more than 2½ years on the <em>New York Times</em> bestseller list. It has been translated into 36 languages and sold more than 6 million copies in the U.S. and 8 million copies worldwide. <em>Bees</em> was named the Book Sense Paperback Book of the Year in 2004, long-listed for the 2002 Orange Prize in England, and won numerous awards.<br /><br /><em>The Mermaid Chair</em> spent 24 weeks on the <em>New York Times</em> hardcover bestseller list, reaching the #1 position, and spent 22 weeks on the <em>New York Times</em> trade paperback list. She is also the author of several acclaimed memoirs, including the <em>New York Times</em> bestseller <em>Traveling with Pomegranates</em>, written with her daughter, Ann Kidd Taylor. Kidd lives in Florida with her husband. <br /><br />Please visit <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.suemonkkidd.com" target="_blank">www.suemonkkidd.com</a> for more information. Follow Sue on Twitter @SueMonkKidd and Facebook <a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/suemonkkidd" target="_blank">https://www.facebook.com/suemonkkidd</a><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7711122023049761996">...more</a>

    </div>
